planet</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:300px;">\n<caption>The five IAU-recognized dwarf planets\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="3"><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" (1801)</center>\n</td>\n<td colspan="3"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_in_True_Color_-_High-Res.jpg"><img alt="Pluto in True Color - High-Res.jpg" data-file-height="8000" (1930)</center>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)"><img alt="Eris and dysnomia2.jpg" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="386" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Inset-kbos13.jpg/200px-Inset-kbos13.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a><center>Haumea (2004)</center>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="6">The five dwarf planets recognized by the IAU:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> as seen from the <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i> spacecraft</a>. It is the only dwarf planet in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> as viewed by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> space probe on July 13, 2015.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> and its moon <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a> viewed by the Hubble Space Telescope.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and its moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S/2015_(136472)_1" title="S/2015 (136472) 1">S/2015 (136472) 1</a> viewed by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>.</li>\n<li>NASA artist\'s impression of <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>dwarf planet</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary-mass_object" title="Planetary-mass object">planetary-mass object</a> that is neither a true <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> nor a <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellite</a>. That is, it is in direct orbit of a star, and is massive enough for its gravity to compress it into a <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatically equilibrious</a> shape (usually a <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a>), but has not <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared the neighborhood</a> of other material around its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term <i>dwarf planet</i> was adopted in 2006 as part of a <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">three-way categorization</a> of bodies orbiting the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup> brought about by an increase in discoveries of <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">objects farther away from the Sun than Neptune</a> that rivaled <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> in size, and finally precipitated by the discovery of an even more massive object, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-2">[2]</a></sup> The exclusion of dwarf planets from the roster of planets by the IAU has been both praised and criticized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2009_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2009-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of July 2008 the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) recognizes five dwarf planets: <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> in the asteroid belt, and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> in the outer Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGPSN_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGPSN-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Only two of these bodies, Ceres and Pluto, have been observed in enough detail to demonstrate that they actually fit the IAU\'s definition. The IAU accepted Eris as a dwarf planet because it is more massive than Pluto. They subsequently decided that unnamed trans-Neptunian objects with an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_(H)" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> brighter than +1 (and hence a diameter of &#8805;838&#160;km assuming a <a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">geometric albedo</a> of &#8804;1)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bruton_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bruton-10">[10]</a></sup> are to be named under the assumption that they are dwarf planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU2008_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU2008-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The classification of bodies in other <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a> with the characteristics of dwarf planets has not been addressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draft_Resolution_5_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Draft_Resolution_5-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Vesta_in_natural_color_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Vesta_in_natural_color_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vesta_in_natural_color_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a>, one of the largest asteroids</div></div></div>\n<p>Starting in 1801, astronomers discovered <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and other bodies between <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and Jupiter which were for decades considered to be planets. Between then and around 1851, when the number of planets had reached 23, astronomers started using the word <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> for the smaller bodies and then stopped naming or classifying them as planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the discovery of Pluto in 1930, most astronomers considered the Solar System to have nine planets, along with thousands of significantly smaller bodies (<a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>). For almost 50&#160;years Pluto was thought to be larger than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> but with the discovery in 1978 of Pluto\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>, it became possible to measure Pluto\'s mass accurately and to determine that it was much smaller than initial estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It was roughly one-twentieth the mass of Mercury, which made Pluto by far the smallest planet. Although it was still more than ten times as massive as the largest object in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, Ceres, it had one-fifth the mass of Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Furthermore, having some unusual characteristics, such as large <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> and a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">orbital inclination</a>, it became evident that it was a different kind of body from any of the other planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1990s, astronomers began to find objects in the same region of space as Pluto (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>), and some even farther away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Many of these shared several of Pluto\'s key orbital characteristics, and Pluto started being seen as the largest member of a new class of objects, <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>. This led some astronomers to stop referring to Pluto as a planet. Several terms, including <i>subplanet</i> and <i>planetoid</i>, started to be used for the bodies now known as dwarf planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> By 2005, three trans-Neptunian objects comparable in size to Pluto (<a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>) had been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It became clear that either they would also have to be classified as planets, or Pluto would have to be reclassified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Astronomers were also confident that more objects as large as Pluto would be discovered, and the number of planets would start growing quickly if Pluto were to remain a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> (then known as <span class="nowrap">2003 UB<sub>313</sub></span>) was discovered in January 2005;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> it was thought to be slightly larger than Pluto, and some reports informally referred to it as the <i><a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">tenth planet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> As a consequence, the issue became a matter of intense debate during the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union#The_XXVIth_General_Assembly_and_the_definition_of_a_planet" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU General Assembly</a> in August 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The IAU\'s initial draft proposal included <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>, Eris, and Ceres in the list of planets. After many astronomers objected to this proposal, an alternative was drawn up by the Uruguayan astronomers <a href="/wiki/Julio_%C3%81ngel_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Julio &#193;ngel Fern&#225;ndez">Julio &#193;ngel Fern&#225;ndez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Tancredi" title="Gonzalo Tancredi">Gonzalo Tancredi</a>: they proposed an intermediate category for objects large enough to be round but which had not cleared their orbits of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>. Dropping Charon from the list, the new proposal also removed Pluto, Ceres, and Eris, because they have not cleared their orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19aug_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-19aug-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IAU\'s final Resolution 5A preserved this three-category system for the celestial bodies orbiting the Sun. It reads:\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8220;\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The IAU&#160;... resolves that planets and other bodies, except satellites, in our Solar System be defined into three distinct categories in the following way: <br/>\n<p>(1) A planet<sup>1</sup> is a celestial body that (a) is in orbit around the Sun, (b) has sufficient mass for its self-gravity to overcome rigid body forces so that it assumes a <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a> (nearly round) shape, and (c) has <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared the neighbourhood</a> around its orbit. <br/>\n(2) A "<i>dwarf planet</i>" is a celestial body that (a) is in orbit around the Sun, (b) has sufficient mass for its self-gravity to overcome rigid body forces so that it assumes a hydrostatic equilibrium (nearly round) shape,<sup>2</sup> (c) has not cleared the neighbourhood around its orbit, and (d) is not a <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">satellite</a>. <br/>\n(3) All other objects,<sup>3</sup> except satellites, orbiting the Sun shall be referred to collectively as "<a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">Small Solar System Bodies</a>." <br/>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><small>Footnotes:</small></dd><small>\n<dd><sup>1</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Planet#Solar_System" title="Planet">eight planets</a> are: <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.</dd>\n<dd><sup>2</sup> An IAU process will be established to assign borderline objects either dwarf planet or other status.</dd>\n</small><dd><small><sup>3</sup> These currently include most of the Solar System asteroids, most Trans-Neptunian Objects (TNOs), comets, and other small bodies.</small></dd></dl>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8221;\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>Although concerns were raised about the classification of planets orbiting other stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draft_Resolution_5_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Draft_Resolution_5-12">[12]</a></sup> the issue was not resolved; it was proposed instead to decide this only when such objects start to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19aug_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-19aug-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="635" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/350px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/525px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/700px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> showing the types of bodies in the Solar System (except the Sun).</div></div></div>\n<p>The term <i>dwarf planet</i> has itself been somewhat controversial, as it could imply that these bodies are planets, much as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_star" title="Dwarf star">dwarf stars</a> are stars. This is the conception of the Solar System that Stern promoted when he coined the phrase. The older word <i>planetoid</i> ("having the form of a planet") has no such connotation, and is also used by astronomers for bodies that fit the IAU definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Brown states that <i>planetoid</i> is "a perfectly good word" that has been used for these bodies for years, and that the use of the term <i>dwarf planet</i> for a non-planet is "dumb", but that it was motivated by an attempt by the IAU division III plenary session to reinstate Pluto as a planet in a second resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killed_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-killed-30">[30]</a></sup> Indeed, the draft of Resolution 5A had called these median bodies <i>planetoids,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> but the plenary session voted unanimously to change the name to <i>dwarf planet.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup> The second resolution, 5B, defined <i>dwarf planets</i> as a subtype of <i>planet</i>, as Stern had originally intended, distinguished from the other eight that were to be called "classical planets". Under this arrangement, the twelve planets of the rejected proposal were to be preserved in a distinction between eight <i>classical planets</i> and four <i>dwarf planets</i>. Resolution 5B was defeated in the same session that 5A was passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killed_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-killed-30">[30]</a></sup> Because of the semantic inconsistency of a <i>dwarf planet</i> not being a planet due to the failure of Resolution 5B, alternative terms such as <i>nanoplanet</i> and <i>subplanet</i> were discussed, but there was no consensus among the CSBN to change it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In most languages equivalent terms have been created by translating <i>dwarf planet</i> more-or-less literally: French <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">plan&#232;te <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nain#French" title="wikt:nain">naine</a></i></i>, Spanish <i><i lang="es" title="Spanish language text">planeta enano</i></i>, German <i><i lang="de" title="German language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Zwerg" title="wikt:Zwerg">Zwergplanet</a></i></i>, Russian <i>karlikovaya planeta</i> (<span lang="ru" title="Russian language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA" title="wikt:&#1050;&#1072;&#1088;&#1083;&#1080;&#1082;">&#1082;&#1072;&#1088;&#1083;&#1080;&#1082;&#1086;&#1074;&#1072;&#1103;</a> &#1087;&#1083;&#1072;&#1085;&#1077;&#1090;&#1072;</span>), Arabic <i>kaukab qazm</i> (<span lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">&#1603;&#1608;&#1603;&#1576; <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%82%D8%B2%D9%85" title="wikt:&#1602;&#1586;&#1605;">&#1602;&#1586;&#1605;</a></span>), Chinese <i>&#462;ix&#237;ngx&#299;ng</i> (<span lang="cmn" title="Mandarin Chinese language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%9F%AE" title="wikt:&#30702;">&#30702;</a>&#34892;&#26143;</span>), Korean <i>waesohangseong</i> (&#50780;&#49548;&#54665;&#49457;; &#30702;&#23567;&#34892;&#26143;), but in Japanese they are called <i>junwakusei</i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">&#28310;&#24785;&#26143;</span>) meaning "subplanets" or "almost-planets".\n</p><p>IAU Resolution 6a of 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resolution_Result_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Resolution_Result-34">[34]</a></sup> recognizes Pluto as "the prototype of a new category of trans-Neptunian objects". The name and precise nature of this category were not specified but left for the IAU to establish at a later date; in the debate leading up to the resolution, the members of the category were variously referred to as <i>plutons</i> and <i>plutonian objects</i> but neither name was carried forward, perhaps due to objections from geologists that this would create confusion with their <i><a href="/wiki/Pluton" title="Pluton">pluton</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 11, 2008, the IAU Executive Committee announced a name, <i><a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a></i>, and a definition: all trans-Neptunian dwarf planets are plutoids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU2008_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAU2008-11">[11]</a></sup> The authority of that initial announcement has not been universally recognized: \n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8220;\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">...in part because of an email miscommunication, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">WG-PSN [Working Group for Planetary System Nomenclature]</a> was not involved in choosing the word plutoid. ... In fact, a vote taken by the WG-PSN subsequent to the Executive Committee meeting has rejected the use of that specific term..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">&#8221;\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>2</sup></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left"><a href="/wiki/Neptune" style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>2</sup></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background: #EEF;">\n<td align="left"><a href="/wiki/Pluto" style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;2</sup></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background: #EEF;">\n<td align="left"><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>\n</td>\n<td>0.0028\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6997213000000000000&#9824;</span>2.13<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;2</sup></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background: #EEF;">\n<td align="left"><a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>\n</td>\n<td>0.00022\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993364000000000000&#9824;</span>3.64<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;7</sup></span>\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998700000000000000&#9824;</span>&lt;0.07</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6996160000000000000&#9824;</span>1.6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;4</sup></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="5" style="font-size: 0.8em; font-weight: normal; text-align: center; padding: 4px;">\n<p>Showing the planets and the largest known sub-planetary objects (purple) covering the orbital zones containing likely dwarf planets. All known possible dwarf planets have smaller discriminants than those shown for that zone.\n</p>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="5">\n<table style="font-size: 0.9em; font-weight: normal; text-align: left; padding: 4px">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td valign="top"><sup>(1)</sup></td>\n<td>Mass in <a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub></a>, the unit of mass equal to that of Earth (5.97 &#215; 10<sup>24</sup> kg).\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td valign="top"><sup>(2)</sup></td>\n<td><i>&#923;</i> is the capacity to <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">clear the neighbourhood</a> (greater than 1 for planets) by Stern and Levison. <span class="texhtml">&#923; = <i>k</i> <i>M</i><sup>2</sup> <i>a</i><sup>&#8722;3/2</sup></span>, where <i>k</i> = 0.0043 for units of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yottagram" title="Yottagram">Yg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>, and <i>a</i> is the body\'s semi-major axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2002_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2002-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td valign="top"><sup>(3)</sup></td>\n<td><i>&#181;</i> is <i>Soter\'s planetary discriminant</i> (greater than 100 for planets). <i>&#181;</i> = <i>M</i>/<i>m</i>, where <i>M</i> is the mass of the body, and <i>m</i> is the aggregate mass of all the other bodies that share its orbital zone.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td valign="top"><sup>(4)</sup></td>\n<td><i>&#928;</i> is the capacity to <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">clear the neighbourhood</a> (greater than 1 for planets) by Margot. <span class="texhtml">&#928; = <i>k</i> <i>M</i> <i>a</i><sup>&#8722;9/8</sup></span>, where <i>k</i> = 807 for units of <a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass">Earth masses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot_2015_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margot_2015-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_dominance">Orbital dominance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbital dominance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">Clearing the neighbourhood</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_F._Levison" title="Harold F. Levison">Harold F. Levison</a> introduced a parameter &#923; (<a href="/wiki/Lambda" title="Lambda">lambda</a>), expressing the likelihood of an encounter resulting in a given deflection of orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2002_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2002-38">[38]</a></sup> The value of this parameter in Stern\'s model is proportional to the square of the mass and inversely proportional to the period. This value can be used to estimate the capacity of a body to <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">clear the neighbourhood</a> of its orbit, where &#923; &gt; 1 will eventually clear it. A gap of five orders of magnitude in &#923; was found between the smallest <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> and the largest asteroids and Kuiper belt objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soter_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-soter-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Using this parameter, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Soter" title="Steven Soter">Steven Soter</a> and other astronomers argued for a distinction between planets and dwarf planets based on the inability of the latter to "clear the neighbourhood around their orbits": planets are able to remove smaller bodies near their orbits by collision, capture, or gravitational disturbance (or establish orbital resonances that prevent collisions), whereas dwarf planets lack the mass to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2002_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2002-38">[38]</a></sup> Soter went on to propose a parameter he called the <i>planetary discriminant</i>, designated with the symbol &#181; (<a href="/wiki/Mu_(letter)" title="Mu (letter)">mu</a>), that represents an experimental measure of the actual degree of cleanliness of the orbital zone (where &#181; is calculated by dividing the mass of the candidate body by the total mass of the other objects that share its orbital zone), where &#181; &gt; 100 is deemed to be cleared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soter_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-soter-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Margot" title="Jean-Luc Margot">Jean-Luc Margot</a> refined Stern and Levison\'s concept to produce a similar parameter &#928; (<a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">Pi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margot_2015_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Margot_2015-39">[39]</a></sup> It is based on theory, avoiding the empirical data used by &#923;. &#928;&#160;&gt;&#160;1 indicates a planet, and there is again a gap of several orders of magnitude between planets and dwarf planets.\n</p><p>There are several other schemes that try to differentiate between planets and dwarf planets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-6">[6]</a></sup> but the 2006 definition uses this concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrostatic_equilibrium">Hydrostatic equilibrium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hydrostatic equilibrium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">Hydrostatic equilibrium</a></div>\n<p>Sufficient internal pressure, caused by the body\'s gravitation, will turn a body <a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">plastic</a>, and sufficient plasticity will allow high elevations to sink and hollows to fill in, a process known as gravitational relaxation. Bodies smaller than a few kilometers are dominated by non-gravitational forces and tend to have an irregular shape. Larger objects, where gravitation is significant but not dominant, are "potato" shaped; the more massive the body is, the higher its internal pressure and the more rounded its shape, until the pressure is sufficient to overcome its internal <a href="/wiki/Compressive_strength" title="Compressive strength">compressive strength</a> and it achieves <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>. At this point a body is as round as it is possible to be, given its rotation and tidal effects, and is an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a> in shape. This is the defining limit of a dwarf planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When an object is in hydrostatic equilibrium, a global layer of liquid covering its surface would form a liquid surface of the same shape as the body, apart from small-scale surface features such as craters and fissures. If the body does not rotate, it will be a sphere, but the faster it rotates, the more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">scalene</a> it becomes. If such a rotating body were to be heated until it melted, its overall shape would not change when liquid. The extreme example of a non-spherical body in hydrostatic equilibrium is <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, which is twice as long along its major axis as it is at the poles. If the body has a massive nearby companion, then tidal forces come into effect as well, distorting it into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prolate_spheroid" title="Prolate spheroid">prolate spheroid</a>. An example of this is Jupiter\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, which is the most volcanically active body in the Solar System due to effects of <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a>. Tidal forces also cause a body\'s rotation to gradually become <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a>, such that it always presents the same face to its companion. An extreme example of this is the Pluto&#8211;Charon system, where both bodies are tidally locked to each other. Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> is also tidally locked, as are many satellites of the gas giants.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="622" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg/300px-Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg/450px-Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg/600px-Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dwarf_planet_masses_relative_to_Moon.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The masses of the IAU-recognized dwarf planets plus Charon relative to the Moon. The mass of Makemake is a rough estimate. (See <a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a> for a graph of several additional likely dwarf planets without Ceres.)</div></div></div>\n<p>The upper and lower size and mass limits of dwarf planets have not been specified by the IAU. There is no defined upper limit, and an object larger or more massive than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> that has not "cleared the neighbourhood around its orbit" would be classified as a dwarf planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The lower limit is determined by the requirements of achieving a hydrostatic equilibrium shape, but the size or mass at which an object attains this shape depends on its composition and thermal history. The original draft of the 2006 IAU resolution redefined hydrostatic equilibrium shape as applying "to objects with mass above 5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>&#160;kg and diameter greater than 800&#160;km",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draft_Resolution_5_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Draft_Resolution_5-12">[12]</a></sup> but this was not retained in the final draft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalresolution_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-finalresolution-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Empirical observations suggest that the lower limit will vary according to the composition and thermal history of the object. For a body made of rigid silicates, such as the stony asteroids, the transition to hydrostatic equilibrium should occur at a diameter of approximately 600&#160;km and a mass of 3.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>&#160;kg. For a body made of less rigid water ice, the limit should be about 320&#160;km and 10<sup>19</sup>&#160;kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole2000_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole2000-42">[42]</a></sup> In the asteroid belt, Ceres is the only body that clearly surpasses the silicaceous limit (though it is actually a rocky&#8211;icy body), and its shape is an equilibrium spheroid. <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a> and <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> are rocky and are just below the limit. Pallas, at 525&#8211;560&#160;km and 1.85&#8211;2.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>&#160;kg, is "nearly round" but still somewhat irregular. Vesta, at 530&#160;km and 2.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>&#160;kg, deviates from an ellipsoid shape primarily due to a large impact basin at its pole.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dwarf_planets_and_possible_dwarf_planets">Dwarf planets and possible dwarf planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dwarf planets and possible dwarf planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg/440px-TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg/660px-TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg/880px-TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheTransneptunians_Size_Albedo_Color.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the relative sizes, <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedos</a>, and colours of the largest trans-Neptunian objects</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2470" data-file-width="5501" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png/400px-Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png/600px-Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png/800px-Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eight_TNO_moonorbits.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eight dwarves or Candidates and moon\'s orbits, shown at angle</div></div></div>\n<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> (TNOs) are thought to have icy cores and therefore would require a diameter of perhaps 400&#160;km (250&#160;mi)&#8212;only about 3% of that of Earth&#8212;to relax into gravitational equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-43">[43]</a></sup> As of  January&#160;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, about 150 known TNOs are considered potential dwarf planets, although only rough estimates of the diameters of most of these objects are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownList_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrownList-44">[44]</a></sup> A team is investigating thirty of these, and think that the number will eventually prove to be around 200 in the Kuiper belt, with thousands more beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognized">Recognized</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Recognized">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The IAU has recognized five bodies as dwarf planets since 2008: Ceres, Pluto, Eris, Haumea, and Makemake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-DP5_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-DP5-45">[45]</a></sup> Ceres and Pluto are known to be dwarf planets through direct observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tancredi2008_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tancredi2008-46">[46]</a></sup> Eris is recognized as a dwarf planet because it is more massive than Pluto (measurements by <i>New Horizons</i>  indicate that Pluto\'s diameter is larger than that of Eris), whereas Haumea and Makemake qualify based on their absolute magnitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGPSN_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-WGPSN-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resolution_Result_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Resolution_Result-34">[34]</a></sup> In relative distance from the Sun, the five are:\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_symbol.svg"><img alt="Ceres symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/15px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/23px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/30px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="15"/></a> &#8211; discovered on January 1, 1801, 45&#160;years before <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. Considered a planet for half a century before reclassification as an asteroid. Accepted as a dwarf planet by the IAU on September 13, 2006.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> &#9799; &#8211; discovered on February 18, 1930. Classified as a planet for 76&#160;years. Reclassified as a dwarf planet by the IAU on August 24, 2006.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a> &#8211; discovered on December 28, 2004. Accepted by the IAU as a dwarf planet on September 17, 2008.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> &#8211; discovered on March 31, 2005. Accepted by the IAU as a dwarf planet on July 11, 2008.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> &#8211; discovered on January 5, 2005. Called the "<a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">tenth planet</a>" in media reports. Accepted by the IAU as a dwarf planet on September 13, 2006.</li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed">Proposed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Proposed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets" title="List of possible dwarf planets">List of possible dwarf planets</a></div>\n<p>Liberal estimates are that another <a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets" title="List of possible dwarf planets">hundred or so known objects</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> may be dwarf planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownList_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrownList-44">[44]</a></sup> Such estimates are that up to 200 dwarf planets will be identified when the entire region known as the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> is explored, and that the number may exceed 10,000 when objects scattered outside the Kuiper belt are considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2012_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2012-47">[47]</a></sup> Individual astronomers recognize several of these,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownList_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrownList-44">[44]</a></sup> and Brown maintains a list of hundreds of candidate objects, ranging from "nearly certain" to "possible" dwarf planets.<i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-free_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-free-48">[48]</a></sup></i> However, it has been proposed that dark, low-density bodies smaller than 900&#8211;1000 km in diameter may retain internal porosity from their formation, never having fully collapsed into solid planetary bodies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy2019_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy2019-49">[49]</a></sup> in which case they could not be dwarf-planets.\n</p><p>As of Feb 2018, Brown\'s list includes 952 objects, identifying ten known <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a>&#8212;the four accepted by the IAU plus the six bodies: <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" title="(307261) 2002 MS4">(307261) 2002 MS<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">4</span></span></a></span> and <a href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" title="120347 Salacia">Salacia</a>&#8212;as "near certain", with another 26 "highly likely".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brownlist2017_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brownlist2017-50">[50]</a></sup> Notably, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span> (1535 km) has a larger diameter than the dwarf planet Makemake (1430 km). Stern states that there are more than a dozen known dwarf planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2012_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2012-47">[47]</a></sup> Grundy et al. cast doubt on the possibility that Salacia, Varda and similar objects are planetary bodies, while accepting that Orcus and Quaoar probably are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy2019_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy2019-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mike Brown considers an additional six trans-Neptunian objects to be "nearly certainly"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownList_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-BrownList-44">[44]</a></sup> dwarf planets with diameters at or above 900 kilometers. These objects are:\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a> &#8211; discovered on June 5, 2002</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(307261)_2002_MS4" title="(307261) 2002 MS4">2002 MS<sub>4</sub></a></span> &#8211; discovered on June 18, 2002</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a> &#8211; discovered on November 14, 2003</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a> &#8211; discovered on February 17, 2004</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/120347_Salacia" title="120347 Salacia">Salacia</a> &#8211; discovered on September 22, 2004</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span> &#8211; discovered on July 17, 2007</li></ol>\n<p>Tancredi et al. advised the IAU to officially accept Orcus, Sedna and Quaoar. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Tancredi" title="Gonzalo Tancredi">Gonzalo Tancredi</a> considers the five TNOs <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">Varuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/28978_Ixion" title="28978 Ixion">Ixion</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(208996)_2003_AZ84" title="(208996) 2003 AZ84">2003 AZ<sub>84</sub></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(90568)_2004_GV9" title="(90568) 2004 GV9">2004 GV<sub>9</sub></a></span>, and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(55565)_2002_AW197" title="(55565) 2002 AW197">2002 AW<sub>197</sub></a></span> to be dwarf planets as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tancredi2008_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tancredi2008-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="background-color: #D6D6FF; margin: 18px auto; border: 1px solid #BDBDFF; padding: 6px;">\n<caption style="font-size: 1.2em;"><b>Objects recognized by the IAU as dwarf style="display:none">7003111000000000000&#9824;</span>1110<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>5</span>\n</td>\n<td>1.8&#8211;2.0%\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000139999999999999&#9824;</span>1.4<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.1</span>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>0.74\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weywot_(moon)" title="Weywot (moon)">1</a>\n</td>\n<td>&#8776; 43\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>2.4\n</td></tr>\n<tr align="center">\n<th align="left"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>\n</th>\n<td>&#8776; 44%\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003125300000000000&#9824;</span>1253<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+43<br/>&#8722;43</span></span></span>\n</td>\n<td>2.4%\n</td>\n<td>1.75\n</td>\n<td>1.72\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>1.87\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>1.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr align="center">\n<th align="left"><a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>\n</th>\n<td>&#8776; 30%\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002995000000000000&#9824;</span>995<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>80</span>\n</td>\n<td>&#8776; 1.4%\n</td>\n<td>&#8776; 1\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>0.42\n</td>\n<td>0\n</td>\n<td>&#8776; 12\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>1.5\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>, the next-most-massive body in the asteroid belt after Ceres, is roughly spherical, deviating mainly because of massive impacts that formed <a href="/wiki/Rheasilvia" title="Rheasilvia">Rheasilvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veneneia" title="Veneneia">Veneneia</a> crater after it solidified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Furthermore, its triaxial dimensions are not consistent with hydrostatic equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asmar_2012_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asmar_2012-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russel_2012_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russel_2012-58">[58]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> is thought to be a captured dwarf planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agnor06_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agnor06-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a> is a captured body that, like Vesta, is no longer in hydrostatic equilibrium, but is thought to have been so early in its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="870" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg/220px-PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg/330px-PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg/440px-PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19562-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-RC3-image19-20150506.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dwarf planet Ceres, as imaged by NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a> spacecraft.</div></div></div>\n<p>On March 6, 2015, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> spacecraft began to orbit <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, becoming the first spacecraft to orbit a dwarf planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150306_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150306-61">[61]</a></sup> On July 14, 2015, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> space probe flew by <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and its five moons. Dawn has also explored the former dwarf planet Vesta. <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a> has been explored by Cassini (most recently) and Voyager 2, which also explored <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>. These three are thought to be former dwarf planets and therefore their exploration helps in the study of the evolution of dwarf planets.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contention">Contention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Contention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the immediate aftermath of the IAU definition of dwarf planet, some scientists expressed their disagreement with the IAU resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-6">[6]</a></sup> Campaigns included car bumper stickers and T-shirts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a> (the discoverer of Eris) agrees with the reduction of the number of planets to eight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA has announced that it will use the new guidelines established by the IAU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a>, the director of <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">NASA\'s mission to Pluto</a>, rejects the current IAU definition of planet, both in terms of defining dwarf planets as something other than a type of planet, and in using orbital characteristics (rather than intrinsic characteristics) of objects to define them as dwarf planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Thus, in 2011, he still referred to Pluto as a planet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and accepted other dwarf planets such as Ceres and Eris, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_planet" title="Satellite planet">larger moons</a>, as additional planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-News.discovery.com_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-News.discovery.com-67">[67]</a></sup> Several years before the IAU definition, he used orbital characteristics to separate "&#252;berplanets" (the dominant eight) from "unterplanets" (the dwarf planets), considering both types "planets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2002_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2002-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary-mass_moons">Planetary-mass moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Planetary-mass moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">List of natural satellites</a></div>\n<p>Nineteen <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> are known to be massive enough to have relaxed into a rounded shape under their own gravity, and seven of them are more massive than either Eris or Pluto. They are not physically distinct from the dwarf planets, but are not dwarf planets because they do not directly orbit the Sun. The seven that are more massive than Eris are the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, the four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> of Jupiter (<a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>), one moon of Saturn (<a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>), and one moon of Neptune (<a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>). The others are six moons of Saturn (<a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a>), five moons of Uranus (<a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>), and one moon of Pluto (<a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>). There are additional possibilities among TNOs, including <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a> orbiting Eris. Alan Stern calls these moons "satellite planets", one of three categories of planet together with dwarf planets and classical planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-News.discovery.com_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-News.discovery.com-67">[67]</a></sup> The term <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">planemo</a></i> ("planetary-mass object") covers all three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a draft resolution for the <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU definition of planet</a>, both Pluto and Charon would have been considered dwarf planets in a binary system, given that they both satisfied the mass and shape requirements for dwarf planets and revolved around a common <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Barycenter_in_astrophysics_and_astronomy" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> located between the two bodies (rather than within one of the bodies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draft_Resolution_5_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Draft_Resolution_5-12">[12]</a></sup> The IAU currently states that Charon is not considered to be a dwarf planet and is just a satellite of Pluto, although the idea that Charon might qualify to be a dwarf planet in its own right may be considered at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> The location of the <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> depends not only on the relative masses of the bodies, but also on the distance between them; the barycenter of the Sun&#8211;Jupiter orbit, for example, lies outside the Sun.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><ul style="font-weight: bold">\n<li><a href="/wiki/Book:Dwarf_Planets_of_the_Solar_System_%26_Their_Satellites" title="Book:Dwarf Planets of the Solar System &amp; Their Satellites">Book: Dwarf Planets of the Solar System &amp; Their Satellites</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Book:Solar_System" title="Book:Solar System">Book: Solar System</a></li>\n</ul></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_astronomical_objects" title="Lists of astronomical objects">Lists of astronomical objects</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">Centaur</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitationally_rounded_objects_of_the_Solar_System" title="List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System">List of gravitationally rounded objects of the Solar System</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_planets" title="Lists of planets">List of planetary bodies</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets" title="List of possible dwarf planets">List of possible dwarf planets</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Planet#Objects_formerly_considered_planets" title="Planet">List of Solar System bodies formerly regarded as planets</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_small_Solar_System_bodies" title="Lists of small Solar System bodies">Lists of small Solar System bodies</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mesoplanet" title="Mesoplanet">Mesoplanet</a></li></ul></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The footnote in the original text reads: <i>For two or more objects comprising a multiple object system.... A secondary object satisfying these conditions i.e. that of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">shape</a> is also designated a planet if the system <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentre" title="Barycentre">barycentre</a> resides outside the primary. Secondary objects not satisfying these criteria are "satellites". Under this definition, Pluto\'s companion Charon is a planet, making Pluto&#8211;Charon a double planet.</i></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dwarf_planet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-finalresolution-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-finalresolution_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">IAU (August 24, 2006). <a class="external text" href="http://www.iau.org/static/resolutions/Resolution_GA26-5-6.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Definition of a Planet in the Solar System: Resolutions 5 and 6"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>IAU 2006 General Assembly</i>.
